</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">The blue graph shows the apparent <i>percentage</i> (not the absolute number) of marine <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animal</a> <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genera</a> becoming extinct during any given time interval. It does not represent all marine species, just those that are readily fossilized. The labels of the traditional "Big Five" extinction events and the more recently recognised <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/End-Capitanian_extinction_event" title="End-Capitanian extinction event">End-Capitanian extinction event</a> are clickable hyperlinks. <i>(<a href="/wiki/File:Extinction_intensity.svg" title="File:Extinction intensity.svg">source and image info</a>)</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>An <b>extinction</b> <b>event</b> (also known as a <b>mass extinction</b> or <b>biotic crisis</b>) is a widespread and rapid decrease in the <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a> on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>. Such an event is identified by a sharp change in the diversity and abundance of <a href="/wiki/Multicellular_organism" title="Multicellular organism">multicellular organisms</a>. It occurs when the rate of <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinction</a> increases with respect to the rate of <a href="/wiki/Speciation" title="Speciation">speciation</a>. Because most diversity and <a href="/wiki/Biomass_(ecology)" title="Biomass (ecology)">biomass</a> on Earth is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microbial" title="Microbial">microbial</a>, and thus difficult to measure, recorded extinction events affect the easily observed, biologically complex component of the <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a> rather than the total diversity and abundance of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">Extinction</a> occurs at an uneven rate. Based on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossil_record" title="Fossil record">fossil record</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Background_extinction_rate" title="Background extinction rate">background rate of extinctions</a> on Earth is about two to five <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpha_taxonomy" title="Alpha taxonomy">taxonomic</a> <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">families</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marine_animal" title="Marine animal">marine animals</a> every million years. Marine fossils are mostly used to measure extinction rates because of their superior fossil record and stratigraphic range compared to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Land_animal" title="Land animal">land animals</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Great_Oxygenation_Event" title="Great Oxygenation Event">Great Oxygenation Event</a> was probably the first major extinction event. Since the <a href="/wiki/Cambrian_explosion" title="Cambrian explosion">Cambrian explosion</a> five further major mass extinctions have significantly exceeded the background extinction rate. The most recent and arguably best-known, the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event</a>, which occurred approximately 66 million years ago (Ma), was a large-scale mass extinction of animal and plant species in a geologically short period of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward_2006_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ward_2006-2">[2]</a></sup> In addition to the five major mass extinctions, there are numerous minor ones as well, and the ongoing mass extinction caused by human activity is sometimes called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sixth_extinction" title="Sixth extinction">sixth extinction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Mass extinctions seem to be a mainly <a href="/wiki/Phanerozoic" title="Phanerozoic">Phanerozoic</a> phenomenon, with extinction rates low before large complex organisms arose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Butterfield2007_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Butterfield2007-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Estimates of the number of major mass extinctions in the last 540 million years range from as few as five to more than twenty. These differences stem from the threshold chosen for describing an extinction event as "major", and the data chosen to measure past diversity.</p>
<p></p>

</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kainops_invius_lateral_and_ventral.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1316" data-file-width="1511" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Kainops_invius_lateral_and_ventral.JPG/220px-Kainops_invius_lateral_and_ventral.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Kainops_invius_lateral_and_ventral.JPG/330px-Kainops_invius_lateral_and_ventral.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Kainops_invius_lateral_and_ventral.JPG/440px-Kainops_invius_lateral_and_ventral.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kainops_invius_lateral_and_ventral.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Trilobites were highly successful marine animals until the Permian–Triassic extinction event wiped them all out</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In a landmark paper published in 1982, <a href="/wiki/Jack_Sepkoski" title="Jack Sepkoski">Jack Sepkoski</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_M._Raup" title="David M. Raup">David M. Raup</a> identified five mass extinctions. They were originally identified as outliers to a general trend of decreasing extinction rates during the Phanerozoic,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alroy2008_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alroy2008-5">[5]</a></sup> but as more stringent statistical tests have been applied to the accumulating data, it has been established that multicellular animal life has experienced five major and many minor mass extinctions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> The "Big Five" cannot be so clearly defined, but rather appear to represent the largest (or some of the largest) of a relatively smooth continuum of extinction events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alroy2008_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Alroy2008-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<ol>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Ordovician%E2%80%93Silurian_extinction_events" title="Ordovician–Silurian extinction events">Ordovician–Silurian extinction events</a></b> (End Ordovician or O–S): 450–440 Ma at the <a href="/wiki/Ordovician" title="Ordovician">Ordovician</a>–<a href="/wiki/Silurian" title="Silurian">Silurian</a> transition. Two events occurred that killed off 27% of all families, 57% of all genera and 60% to 70% of all species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ucr_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-ucr-7">[7]</a></sup> Together they are ranked by many scientists as the second largest of the five major extinctions in Earth's history in terms of percentage of <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genera</a> that became extinct.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Late_Devonian_extinction" title="Late Devonian extinction">Late Devonian extinction</a></b>: 375–360 Ma near the <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">Devonian</a>–<a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a> transition. At the end of the <a href="/wiki/Frasnian" title="Frasnian">Frasnian Age</a> in the later part(s) of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Devonian_Period" title="Devonian Period">Devonian Period</a>, a prolonged series of extinctions eliminated about 19% of all families, 50% of all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genera" title="Genera">genera</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ucr_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-ucr-7">[7]</a></sup> and at least 70% of all species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> This extinction event lasted perhaps as long as 20 million years, and there is evidence for a series of extinction pulses within this period.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Permian%E2%80%93Triassic_extinction_event" title="Permian–Triassic extinction event">Permian–Triassic extinction event</a></b> (End Permian): 252 Ma at the <a href="/wiki/Permian" title="Permian">Permian</a>–<a href="/wiki/Triassic" title="Triassic">Triassic</a> transition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20170216_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20170216-9">[9]</a></sup> Earth's largest extinction killed 57% of all families, 83% of all genera and 90% to 96% of all species<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ucr_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-ucr-7">[7]</a></sup> (53% of marine families, 84% of marine genera, about 96% of all marine species and an estimated 70% of land species,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward_2006_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ward_2006-2">[2]</a></sup> including <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Labandeira_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Labandeira-10">[10]</a></sup> The highly successful marine arthropod, the <a href="/wiki/Trilobite" title="Trilobite">trilobite</a> became extinct. The evidence regarding <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a> is less clear, but new taxa became dominant after the extinction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McElwain2007_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-McElwain2007-11">[11]</a></sup> The "Great Dying" had enormous evolutionary significance: on land, it ended the primacy of <a href="/wiki/Synapsid" title="Synapsid">mammal-like reptiles</a>. The recovery of vertebrates took 30 million years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> but the vacant <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">niches</a> created the opportunity for <a href="/wiki/Archosaur" title="Archosaur">archosaurs</a> to <a href="/wiki/Archosaur#Archosaur_takeover_in_the_Triassic" title="Archosaur">become ascendant</a>. In the seas, the percentage of animals that were <a href="/wiki/Sessility_(motility)" title="Sessility (motility)">sessile</a> dropped from 67% to 50%. The whole late Permian was a difficult time for at least marine life, even before the "Great Dying".</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Triassic%E2%80%93Jurassic_extinction_event" title="Triassic–Jurassic extinction event">Triassic–Jurassic extinction event</a></b> (End Triassic): 201.3 Ma at the <a href="/wiki/Triassic" title="Triassic">Triassic</a>–<a href="/wiki/Jurassic" title="Jurassic">Jurassic</a> transition. About 23% of all families, 48% of all genera (20% of marine families and 55% of marine genera) and 70% to 75% of all species became extinct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ucr_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-ucr-7">[7]</a></sup> Most non-dinosaurian <a href="/wiki/Archosaur" title="Archosaur">archosaurs</a>, most <a href="/wiki/Therapsid" title="Therapsid">therapsids</a>, and most of the large <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a> were eliminated, leaving <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur">dinosaurs</a> with little terrestrial competition. Non-dinosaurian archosaurs continued to dominate aquatic environments, while <a href="/wiki/Diapsid#Taxonomy" title="Diapsid">non-archosaurian diapsids</a> continued to dominate marine environments. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temnospondyl" title="Temnospondyl">Temnospondyl</a> lineage of large amphibians also survived until the Cretaceous in Australia (e.g., <i><a href="/wiki/Koolasuchus" title="Koolasuchus">Koolasuchus</a></i>).</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event</a></b> (End Cretaceous, K–Pg extinction, or formerly K–T extinction): 66 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annum#SI_prefix_multipliers" title="Annum">Ma</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a> (<a href="/wiki/Maastrichtian" title="Maastrichtian">Maastrichtian</a>) – <a href="/wiki/Paleogene" title="Paleogene">Paleogene</a> (<a href="/wiki/Danian" title="Danian">Danian</a>) transition interval.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> The event formerly called the Cretaceous-Tertiary or K–T extinction or K–T boundary is now officially named the Cretaceous–Paleogene (or K–Pg) extinction event. About 17% of all families, 50% of all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genera" title="Genera">genera</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ucr_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-ucr-7">[7]</a></sup> and 75% of all species became extinct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raup_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Raup-14">[14]</a></sup> In the seas all the <a href="/wiki/Ammonoidea" title="Ammonoidea">ammonites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sauropterygia" title="Sauropterygia">plesiosaurs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mosasaur" title="Mosasaur">mosasaurs</a> disappeared and the percentage of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sessility_(zoology)" title="Sessility (zoology)">sessile</a> animals (those unable to move about) was reduced to about 33%. All non-avian <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur">dinosaurs</a> became extinct during that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The boundary event was severe with a significant amount of variability in the rate of extinction between and among different <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clades</a>. <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">Mammals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a>, the latter descended from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theropod" title="Theropod">theropod</a> dinosaurs, emerged as dominant large land animals.</li>
</ol>
<p>Despite the popularization of these five events, there is no definite line separating them from other extinction events; using different methods of calculating an extinction's impact can lead to other events featuring in the top five.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGhee2011_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-McGhee2011-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>The older the fossil record gets, the more difficult it is to read. This is because:</p>
<ul>
<li>Older fossils are harder to find as they are usually buried at a considerable depth.</li>
<li>Dating older fossils is more difficult.</li>
<li>Productive fossil beds are researched more than unproductive ones, therefore leaving certain periods unresearched.</li>
<li>Prehistoric environmental events can disturb the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deposition_(sediment)" title="Deposition (sediment)">deposition</a> process.</li>
<li>The preservation of fossils varies on land, but marine fossils tend to be better preserved than their sought after land-based counterparts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sole_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-sole-17">[17]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>It has been suggested that the apparent variations in marine biodiversity may actually be an artifact, with abundance estimates directly related to quantity of rock available for sampling from different time periods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> However, statistical analysis shows that this can only account for 50% of the observed pattern,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and other evidence (such as fungal spikes)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (March 2008)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> provides reassurance that most widely accepted extinction events are real. A quantification of the rock exposure of Western Europe indicates that many of the minor events for which a biological explanation has been sought are most readily explained by <a href="/wiki/Sampling_bias" title="Sampling bias">sampling bias</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith2007_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith2007-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Research completed after the seminal 1982 paper has concluded that a sixth mass extinction event is ongoing:</p>
<dl>
<dd>6. <b><a href="/wiki/Holocene_extinction" title="Holocene extinction">Holocene extinction</a></b>: Currently ongoing. Extinctions have occurred at over 1000 times the <a href="/wiki/Background_extinction_rate" title="Background extinction rate">background extinction rate</a> since 1900.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> The mass extinction is considered a result of <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">human activity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sutter_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sutter-24">[24]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>More recent research has indicated that the <b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/End-Capitanian_extinction_event" title="End-Capitanian extinction event">End-Capitanian extinction event</a></b> likely constitutes a separate extinction event from the Permian–Triassic extinction event; if so, it would be larger than many of the "Big Five" extinction events.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolutionary_importance">Evolutionary importance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction_event&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Evolutionary importance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="toccolours searchaux" id="Container" style="text-align: left; padding:0.5em 0 0 0; border-style:solid; float:right;clear:right; padding:0 0.5em; margin:0.3em 0 0.8em 1.4em; overflow: hidden;">

<div class="toccolours" id="Legend" style="margin-left:3.1em; border-style:none; float:left; clear:both;"></div>
<div class="toccolours" id="Caption" style="margin:0 0.5em; float:left; padding:0 auto; border-style:none; clear:both; text-align:center; width:20.5em"><a href="/wiki/Cartesian_coordinate_system#One_dimension" title="Cartesian coordinate system">Axis scale</a>: <a href="/wiki/Geologic_time_scale" title="Geologic time scale">millions of years</a>.<br/>
<small><span style="font:bold 10px Arial;display:inline;background-color:rgba(255,165,0,0.5);padding:0px 4px 0px 4px;"><span style="color:black;">Orange labels:</span> <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_glaciation" title="Timeline of glaciation"><span style="color:black;">known <i><b>ice ages</b></i>.</span></a></span></small><br/>
<small>Also see: <i><a href="/wiki/Template:Human_timeline" title="Template:Human timeline">Human timeline</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Template:Nature_timeline" title="Template:Nature timeline">Nature timeline</a></i></small></div>
</div>
<p>Mass extinctions have sometimes accelerated the <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a> of <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life on Earth</a>. When dominance of particular ecological niches passes from one group of organisms to another, it is rarely because the new dominant group is "superior" to the old and usually because an extinction event eliminates the old dominant group and makes way for the new one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>For example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mammaliformes" title="Mammaliformes">mammaliformes</a> ("almost mammals") and then <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> existed throughout the reign of the <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur">dinosaurs</a>, but could not compete for the large terrestrial vertebrate niches which dinosaurs monopolized. The <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">end-Cretaceous</a> mass extinction removed the non-avian dinosaurs and made it possible for mammals to expand into the large terrestrial vertebrate niches. Ironically, the dinosaurs themselves had been beneficiaries of a previous mass extinction, the <a href="/wiki/Triassic%E2%80%93Jurassic_extinction_event" title="Triassic–Jurassic extinction event">end-Triassic</a>, which eliminated most of their chief rivals, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crurotarsans" title="Crurotarsans">crurotarsans</a>.</p>
<p>Another point of view put forward in the <a href="/wiki/Escalation_hypothesis" title="Escalation hypothesis">Escalation hypothesis</a> predicts that species in ecological niches with more organism-to-organism conflict will be less likely to survive extinctions. This is because the very traits that keep a species numerous and viable under fairly static conditions become a burden once population levels fall among competing organisms during the dynamics of an extinction event.</p>
<p>Furthermore, many groups which survive mass extinctions do not recover in numbers or diversity, and many of these go into long-term decline, and these are often referred to as "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dead_Clade_Walking" title="Dead Clade Walking">Dead Clades Walking</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>Darwin was firmly of the opinion that biotic interactions, such as competition for food and space—the ‘struggle for existence’—were of considerably greater importance in promoting evolution and extinction than changes in the physical environment. He expressed this in <i>The Origin of Species</i>: "Species are produced and exterminated by slowly acting causes…and the most import of all causes of organic change is one which is almost independent of altered…physical conditions, namely the mutual relation of organism to organism-the improvement of one organism entailing the improvement or extermination of others".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Patterns_in_frequency">Patterns in frequency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction_event&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Patterns in frequency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>It has been suggested variously that extinction events occurred periodically, every 26 to 30 million years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raup1984_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Raup1984-50">[50]</a></sup> or that diversity fluctuates episodically every ~62 million years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rohde2005_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rohde2005-51">[51]</a></sup> Various ideas attempt to explain the supposed pattern, including the presence of a <a href="/wiki/Nemesis_(hypothetical_star)" title="Nemesis (hypothetical star)">hypothetical companion star</a> to the sun,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> oscillations in the galactic plane, or passage through the Milky Way's spiral arms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gillmana2008_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gillmana2008-54">[54]</a></sup> However, other authors have concluded the data on marine mass extinctions do not fit with the idea that mass extinctions are periodic, or that ecosystems gradually build up to a point at which a mass extinction is inevitable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alroy2008_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Alroy2008-5">[5]</a></sup> Many of the proposed correlations have been argued to be spurious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Others have argued that there is strong evidence supporting periodicity in a variety of records,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Melott2011_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Melott2011-57">[57]</a></sup> and additional evidence in the form of coincident periodic variation in nonbiological geochemical variables.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Melott_et_al._2012_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Melott_et_al._2012-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<div class="noresize thumb tright" style=";">
<div class="thumbinner" style="overflow:hidden;width:302px;">
<div class="thumbimage" style="height:182px; overflow:hidden; position:relative; background-color:white;">
<div style=";left:0px; top:0px; width:300px; position:absolute;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phanerozoic_biodiversity_blank_01.png" title="Phanerozoic biodiversity as shown by the fossil record"><img alt="" data-file-height="464" data-file-width="784" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Phanerozoic_biodiversity_blank_01.png/300px-Phanerozoic_biodiversity_blank_01.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Phanerozoic_biodiversity_blank_01.png/450px-Phanerozoic_biodiversity_blank_01.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Phanerozoic_biodiversity_blank_01.png/600px-Phanerozoic_biodiversity_blank_01.png 2x" width="300"/></a></div>

<div id="annotation_20x0" style="position:absolute; left:20px; top:0px; line-height:110%"><span style="background-color:transparent;">All <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genera</a></span></div>
<div id="annotation_20x14" style="position:absolute; left:20px; top:14px; line-height:110%"><span style="background-color:transparent;">"Well-defined" genera</span></div>
<div id="annotation_20x28" style="position:absolute; left:20px; top:28px; line-height:110%"><span style="background-color:transparent;">Trend line</span></div>
<div id="annotation_20x42" style="position:absolute; left:20px; top:42px; line-height:110%"><span style="background-color:transparent;">"Big Five" <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_extinction" title="Mass extinction">mass extinctions</a></span></div>

<div id="annotation_50x165" style="position:absolute; left:50px; top:165px; line-height:110%"><span style="background-color:transparent;">Million years ago</span></div>
<div id="annotation_180x165" style="position:absolute; left:180px; top:165px; line-height:110%"><span style="background-color:transparent;">Thousands of genera</span></div>
</div>
<div style="visibility:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phanerozoic_biodiversity_blank_01.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="464" data-file-width="784" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Phanerozoic_biodiversity_blank_01.png/300px-Phanerozoic_biodiversity_blank_01.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Phanerozoic_biodiversity_blank_01.png/450px-Phanerozoic_biodiversity_blank_01.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Phanerozoic_biodiversity_blank_01.png/600px-Phanerozoic_biodiversity_blank_01.png 2x" width="300"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Phanerozoic_biodiversity_blank_01.png" title="File:Phanerozoic biodiversity blank 01.png"></a></div>
Phanerozoic biodiversity as shown by the fossil record</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Mass extinctions are thought to result when a long-term stress is compounded by a short term shock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arens2008_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arens2008-59">[59]</a></sup> Over the course of the <a href="/wiki/Phanerozoic" title="Phanerozoic">Phanerozoic</a>, individual taxa appear to be less likely to become extinct at any time,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang2008_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wang2008-60">[60]</a></sup> which may reflect more robust food webs as well as less extinction-prone species and other factors such as continental distribution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang2008_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wang2008-60">[60]</a></sup> However, even after accounting for sampling bias, there does appear to be a gradual decrease in extinction and origination rates during the Phanerozoic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alroy2008_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-Alroy2008-5">[5]</a></sup> This may represent the fact that groups with higher turnover rates are more likely to become extinct by chance; or it may be an artefact of taxonomy: families tend to become more speciose, therefore less prone to extinction, over time;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alroy2008_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-Alroy2008-5">[5]</a></sup> and larger taxonomic groups (by definition) appear earlier in geological time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Budd2003_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Budd2003-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>It has also been suggested that the oceans have gradually become more hospitable to life over the last 500 million years, and thus less vulnerable to mass extinctions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[note 1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup> but susceptibility to extinction at a taxonomic level does not appear to make mass extinctions more or less probable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang2008_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wang2008-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">Causes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction_event&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Causes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There is still debate about the causes of all mass extinctions. In general, large extinctions may result when a biosphere under long-term stress undergoes a short-term shock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arens2008_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Arens2008-59">[59]</a></sup> An underlying mechanism appears to be present in the correlation of extinction and origination rates to diversity. High diversity leads to a persistent increase in extinction rate; low diversity to a persistent increase in origination rate. These presumably ecologically controlled relationships likely amplify smaller perturbations (asteroid impacts, etc.) to produce the global effects observed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alroy2008_5-5"><a href="#cite_note-Alroy2008-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Identifying_causes_of_particular_mass_extinctions">Identifying causes of particular mass extinctions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction_event&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Identifying causes of particular mass extinctions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A good theory for a particular mass extinction should: (i) explain all of the losses, not just focus on a few groups (such as dinosaurs); (ii) explain why particular groups of organisms died out and why others survived; (iii) provide mechanisms which are strong enough to cause a mass extinction but not a total extinction; (iv) be based on events or processes that can be shown to have happened, not just inferred from the extinction.</p>
<p>It may be necessary to consider combinations of causes. For example, the marine aspect of the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">end-Cretaceous</a> extinction appears to have been caused by several processes which partially overlapped in time and may have had different levels of significance in different parts of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>Arens and West (2006) proposed a "press / pulse" model in which mass extinctions generally require two types of cause: long-term pressure on the eco-system ("press") and a sudden catastrophe ("pulse") towards the end of the period of pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup> Their statistical analysis of marine extinction rates throughout the <a href="/wiki/Phanerozoic" title="Phanerozoic">Phanerozoic</a> suggested that neither long-term pressure alone nor a catastrophe alone was sufficient to cause a significant increase in the extinction rate.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Most_widely_supported_explanations">Most widely supported explanations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction_event&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Most widely supported explanations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Macleod (2001)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup> summarized the relationship between mass extinctions and events which are most often cited as causes of mass extinctions, using data from Courtillot <i>et al.</i> (1996),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup> Hallam (1992)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup> and Grieve <i>et al.</i> (1996):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Flood_basalt" title="Flood basalt">Flood basalt</a> events: 11 occurrences, all associated with significant extinctions<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-i_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-i-71">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ii_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-ii-72">[71]</a></sup> But Wignall (2001) concluded that only five of the major extinctions coincided with flood basalt eruptions and that the main phase of extinctions started before the eruptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup></li>
<li>Sea-level falls: 12, of which seven were associated with significant extinctions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ii_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-ii-72">[71]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Impact_event" title="Impact event">Asteroid impacts</a>: one large impact is associated with a mass extinction, i.e. the Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event; there have been many smaller impacts but they are not associated with significant extinctions.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
</ul>
<p>The most commonly suggested causes of mass extinctions are listed below.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Flood_basalt_events">Flood basalt events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction_event&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Flood basalt events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The formation of <a href="/wiki/Large_igneous_province" title="Large igneous province">large igneous provinces</a> by flood basalt events could have:</p>
<ul>
<li>produced dust and <a href="/wiki/Particulates" title="Particulates">particulate</a> aerosols which inhibited photosynthesis and thus caused <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">food chains</a> to collapse both on land and at sea<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup></li>
<li>emitted sulfur oxides which were precipitated as <a href="/wiki/Acid_rain" title="Acid rain">acid rain</a> and poisoned many organisms, contributing further to the collapse of food chains</li>
<li>emitted <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and thus possibly causing <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">sustained global warming</a> once the dust and particulate aerosols dissipated.</li>
</ul>
<p>Flood basalt events occur as pulses of activity punctuated by dormant periods. As a result, they are likely to cause the climate to oscillate between cooling and warming, but with an overall trend towards warming as the carbon dioxide they emit can stay in the atmosphere for hundreds of years.</p>
<p>It is speculated that massive volcanism caused or contributed to the <a href="/wiki/Permian%E2%80%93Triassic_extinction_event" title="Permian–Triassic extinction event">End-Permian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Triassic%E2%80%93Jurassic_extinction_event" title="Triassic–Jurassic extinction event">End-Triassic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">End-Cretaceous</a> extinctions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup> The correlation between gigantic volcanic events expressed in the large igneous provinces and mass extinctions was shown for the last 260 Myr.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup> Recently such possible correlation was extended for the whole Phanerozoic Eon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sea-level_falls">Sea-level falls</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction_event&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Sea-level falls">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>These are often clearly marked by worldwide sequences of contemporaneous sediments which show all or part of a transition from sea-bed to tidal zone to beach to dry land – and where there is no evidence that the rocks in the relevant areas were raised by geological processes such as <a href="/wiki/Orogeny" title="Orogeny">orogeny</a>. Sea-level falls could reduce the continental shelf area (the most productive part of the oceans) sufficiently to cause a marine mass extinction, and could disrupt weather patterns enough to cause extinctions on land. But sea-level falls are very probably the result of other events, such as sustained global cooling or the sinking of the <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">mid-ocean ridges</a>.</p>
<p>Sea-level falls are associated with most of the mass extinctions, including all of the "Big Five"—<a href="/wiki/Ordovician%E2%80%93Silurian_extinction_events" title="Ordovician–Silurian extinction events">End-Ordovician</a>, <a href="/wiki/Late_Devonian_extinction" title="Late Devonian extinction">Late Devonian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Permian%E2%80%93Triassic_extinction_event" title="Permian–Triassic extinction event">End-Permian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Triassic%E2%80%93Jurassic_extinction_event" title="Triassic–Jurassic extinction event">End-Triassic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">End-Cretaceous</a>.</p>
<p>A study, published in the journal Nature (online June 15, 2008) established a relationship between the speed of mass extinction events and changes in sea level and sediment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peters2008_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peters2008-79">[78]</a></sup> The study suggests changes in ocean environments related to sea level exert a driving influence on rates of extinction, and generally determine the composition of life in the oceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_events">Impact events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction_event&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Impact events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The impact of a sufficiently large asteroid or comet could have caused <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">food chains</a> to collapse both on land and at sea by producing dust and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_particulate_matter" title="Atmospheric particulate matter">particulate</a> aerosols and thus inhibiting photosynthesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup> Impacts on <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>-rich rocks could have emitted sulfur oxides precipitating as poisonous <a href="/wiki/Acid_rain" title="Acid rain">acid rain</a>, contributing further to the collapse of food chains. Such impacts could also have caused <a href="/wiki/Megatsunami" title="Megatsunami">megatsunamis</a> and/or global <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forest_fire" title="Forest fire">forest fires</a>.</p>
<p>Most paleontologists now agree that an asteroid did hit the Earth about 66 Ma ago, but there is an ongoing dispute whether the impact was the sole cause of the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keller_2009_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keller_2009-82">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morgan_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan-83">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sustained_and_significant_global_cooling">Sustained and significant global cooling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction_event&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Sustained and significant global cooling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Sustained global cooling could kill many <a href="/wiki/Polar_circle" title="Polar circle">polar</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperate" title="Temperate">temperate</a> species and force others to migrate towards the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a>; reduce the area available for <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropical</a> species; often make the Earth's climate more arid on average, mainly by locking up more of the planet's water in ice and snow. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glaciation" title="Glaciation">glaciation</a> cycles of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Current_ice_age" title="Current ice age">current ice age</a> are believed to have had only a very mild impact on biodiversity, so the mere existence of a significant cooling is not sufficient on its own to explain a mass extinction.</p>
<p>It has been suggested that global cooling caused or contributed to the <a href="/wiki/Ordovician%E2%80%93Silurian_extinction_events" title="Ordovician–Silurian extinction events">End-Ordovician</a>, <a href="/wiki/Permian%E2%80%93Triassic_extinction_event" title="Permian–Triassic extinction event">Permian–Triassic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Late_Devonian_extinction" title="Late Devonian extinction">Late Devonian</a> extinctions, and possibly others. Sustained global cooling is distinguished from the temporary climatic effects of flood basalt events or impacts.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sustained_and_significant_global_warming">Sustained and significant global warming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction_event&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Sustained and significant global warming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>This would have the opposite effects: expand the area available for <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropical</a> species; kill <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperate" title="Temperate">temperate</a> species or force them to migrate towards the <a href="/wiki/Polar_circle" title="Polar circle">poles</a>; possibly cause severe extinctions of polar species; often make the Earth's climate wetter on average, mainly by melting ice and snow and thus increasing the volume of the <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">water cycle</a>. It might also cause anoxic events in the oceans (see below).</p>
<p>Global warming as a cause of mass extinction is supported by several recent studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>The most dramatic example of sustained warming is the <a href="/wiki/Paleocene%E2%80%93Eocene_Thermal_Maximum" title="Paleocene–Eocene Thermal Maximum">Paleocene–Eocene Thermal Maximum</a>, which was associated with one of the smaller mass extinctions. It has also been suggested to have caused the <a href="/wiki/Triassic%E2%80%93Jurassic_extinction_event" title="Triassic–Jurassic extinction event">Triassic–Jurassic extinction event</a>, during which 20% of all marine families became extinct. Furthermore, the <a href="/wiki/Permian%E2%80%93Triassic_extinction_event" title="Permian–Triassic extinction event">Permian–Triassic extinction event</a> has been suggested to have been caused by warming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Clathrate_gun_hypothesis">Clathrate gun hypothesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction_event&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Clathrate gun hypothesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Clathrate_gun_hypothesis" title="Clathrate gun hypothesis">Clathrate gun hypothesis</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clathrates" title="Clathrates">Clathrates</a> are composites in which a lattice of one substance forms a cage around another. <a href="/wiki/Methane_clathrate" title="Methane clathrate">Methane clathrates</a> (in which water molecules are the cage) form on <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf" title="Continental shelf">continental shelves</a>. These clathrates are likely to break up rapidly and release the methane if the temperature rises quickly or the pressure on them drops quickly—for example in response to sudden <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> or a sudden drop in sea level or even <a href="/wiki/Earthquake" title="Earthquake">earthquakes</a>. Methane is a much more powerful <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">greenhouse</a> gas than carbon dioxide, so a methane eruption ("clathrate gun") could cause rapid global warming or make it much more severe if the eruption was itself caused by global warming.</p>
<p>The most likely signature of such a methane eruption would be a sudden decrease in the <a href="/wiki/Isotope_analysis" title="Isotope analysis">ratio of carbon-13 to carbon-12</a> in sediments, since methane clathrates are low in carbon-13; but the change would have to be very large, as other events can also reduce the percentage of carbon-13.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>It has been suggested that "clathrate gun" methane eruptions were involved in the <a href="/wiki/Permian%E2%80%93Triassic_extinction_event" title="Permian–Triassic extinction event">end-Permian extinction</a> ("the Great Dying") and in the <a href="/wiki/Paleocene%E2%80%93Eocene_Thermal_Maximum" title="Paleocene–Eocene Thermal Maximum">Paleocene–Eocene Thermal Maximum</a>, which was associated with one of the smaller mass extinctions.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Anoxic_events">Anoxic events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction_event&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Anoxic events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Anoxic_event" title="Anoxic event">Anoxic events</a> are situations in which the middle and even the upper layers of the ocean become deficient or totally lacking in oxygen. Their causes are complex and controversial, but all known instances are associated with severe and sustained global warming, mostly caused by sustained massive volcanism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>It has been suggested that anoxic events caused or contributed to the <a href="/wiki/Ordovician%E2%80%93Silurian_extinction_events" title="Ordovician–Silurian extinction events">Ordovician–Silurian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Late_Devonian_extinction" title="Late Devonian extinction">late Devonian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Permian%E2%80%93Triassic_extinction_event" title="Permian–Triassic extinction event">Permian–Triassic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Triassic%E2%80%93Jurassic_extinction_event" title="Triassic–Jurassic extinction event">Triassic–Jurassic</a> extinctions, as well as a number of lesser extinctions (such as the <a href="/wiki/Ireviken_event" title="Ireviken event">Ireviken</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mulde_event" title="Mulde event">Mulde</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lau_event" title="Lau event">Lau</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toarcian_turnover" title="Toarcian turnover">Toarcian</a> and Cenomanian–Turonian events). On the other hand, there are widespread black shale beds from the mid-Cretaceous which indicate anoxic events but are not associated with mass extinctions.</p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bio-availability" title="Bio-availability">bio-availability</a> of <a href="/wiki/Essential_(biology)" title="Essential (biology)">essential</a> <a href="/wiki/Trace_element" title="Trace element">trace elements</a> (in particular <a href="/wiki/Selenium" title="Selenium">selenium</a>) to potentially lethal lows has been shown to coincide with, and likely have contributed to, at least three mass extinction events in the oceans, i.e. at the end of the Ordovician, during the Middle and Late Devonian, and at the end of the Triassic. During periods of low oxygen concentrations very soluble <a href="/wiki/Selenate" title="Selenate">selenate</a> (Se<sup>6+</sup>) is converted into much less soluble <a href="/wiki/Selenide" title="Selenide">selenide</a> (Se<sup>2+</sup>), elemental Se and organo-selenium complexes. Bio-availability of selenium during these extinction events dropped to about 1% of the current oceanic concentration, a level that has been proven lethal to many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extant_taxon" title="Extant taxon">extant</a> organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrogen_sulfide_emissions_from_the_seas">Hydrogen sulfide emissions from the seas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction_event&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Hydrogen sulfide emissions from the seas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Kump, Pavlov and Arthur (2005) have proposed that during the <a href="/wiki/Permian%E2%80%93Triassic_extinction_event" title="Permian–Triassic extinction event">Permian–Triassic extinction event</a> the warming also upset the oceanic balance between <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesising</a> plankton and deep-water <a href="/wiki/Sulfate-reducing_bacteria" title="Sulfate-reducing bacteria">sulfate-reducing bacteria</a>, causing massive emissions of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> which poisoned life on both land and sea and severely weakened the <a href="/wiki/Ozone_layer" title="Ozone layer">ozone layer</a>, exposing much of the life that still remained to fatal levels of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UV_radiation" title="UV radiation">UV radiation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Oceanic_overturn">Oceanic overturn</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction_event&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Oceanic overturn">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Oceanic overturn is a disruption of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermo-haline_circulation" title="Thermo-haline circulation">thermo-haline circulation</a> which lets surface water (which is more saline than deep water because of evaporation) sink straight down, bringing anoxic deep water to the surface and therefore killing most of the oxygen-breathing organisms which inhabit the surface and middle depths. It may occur either at the beginning or the end of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glaciation" title="Glaciation">glaciation</a>, although an overturn at the start of a glaciation is more dangerous because the preceding warm period will have created a larger volume of anoxic water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>Unlike other oceanic catastrophes such as regressions (sea-level falls) and anoxic events, overturns do not leave easily identified "signatures" in rocks and are theoretical consequences of researchers' conclusions about other climatic and marine events.</p>
<p>It has been suggested that oceanic overturn caused or contributed to the <a href="/wiki/Late_Devonian_extinction" title="Late Devonian extinction">late Devonian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Permian%E2%80%93Triassic_extinction_event" title="Permian–Triassic extinction event">Permian–Triassic</a> extinctions.</p>
<h4><span id="A_nearby_nova,_supernova_or_gamma_ray_burst"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="A_nearby_nova.2C_supernova_or_gamma_ray_burst">A nearby nova, supernova or gamma ray burst</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction_event&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: A nearby nova, supernova or gamma ray burst">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A nearby <a href="/wiki/Gamma-ray_burst" title="Gamma-ray burst">gamma-ray burst</a> (less than 6000 <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-years</a> away) would be powerful enough to destroy the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Ozone_layer" title="Ozone layer">ozone layer</a>, leaving organisms vulnerable to <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet radiation</a> from the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20_ways_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-20_ways-95">[94]</a></sup> Gamma ray bursts are fairly rare, occurring only a few times in a given galaxy per million years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup> It has been suggested that a supernova or gamma ray burst caused the <a href="/wiki/Ordovician%E2%80%93Silurian_extinction_events" title="Ordovician–Silurian extinction events">End-Ordovician</a> extinction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Melott_.26_Thomas_2009_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Melott_.26_Thomas_2009-97">[96]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Geomagnetic_reversal">Geomagnetic reversal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction_event&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Geomagnetic reversal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>One theory is that periods of increased <a href="/wiki/Geomagnetic_reversal" title="Geomagnetic reversal">geomagnetic reversals</a> will weaken <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth's magnetic field">Earth's magnetic field</a> long enough to expose the atmosphere to the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar winds</a>, causing oxygen ions to escape the atmosphere in a rate increased by 3–4 orders, resulting in a disastrous decrease in oxygen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Plate_tectonics">Plate tectonics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction_event&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Plate tectonics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Movement of the continents into some configurations can cause or contribute to extinctions in several ways: by initiating or ending <a href="/wiki/Ice_age" title="Ice age">ice ages</a>; by changing ocean and wind currents and thus altering climate; by opening seaways or land bridges which expose previously isolated species to competition for which they are poorly adapted (for example, the extinction of most of South America's <a href="/wiki/Meridiungulata" title="Meridiungulata">native ungulates</a> and all of its <a href="/wiki/Sparassodonta" title="Sparassodonta">large metatherians</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Great_American_Interchange" title="Great American Interchange">creation of a land bridge between North and South America</a>). Occasionally continental drift creates a super-continent which includes the vast majority of Earth's land area, which in addition to the effects listed above is likely to reduce the total area of <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf" title="Continental shelf">continental shelf</a> (the most species-rich part of the ocean) and produce a vast, arid continental interior which may have extreme seasonal variations.</p>
<p>Another theory is that the creation of the super-continent <a href="/wiki/Pangaea" title="Pangaea">Pangaea</a> contributed to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Permian-Triassic_extinction_event" title="Permian-Triassic extinction event">End-Permian</a> mass extinction. Pangaea was almost fully formed at the transition from mid-Permian to late-Permian, and the "Marine genus diversity" diagram at the top of this article shows a level of extinction starting at that time which might have qualified for inclusion in the "Big Five" if it were not overshadowed by the "Great Dying" at the end of the Permian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_hypotheses">Other hypotheses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction_event&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Other hypotheses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Many other hypotheses have been proposed, such as the spread of a new disease, or simple out-competition following an especially successful biological innovation. But all have been rejected, usually for one of the following reasons: they require events or processes for which there is no evidence; they assume mechanisms which are contrary to the available evidence; they are based on other theories which have been rejected or superseded.</p>
<p>Scientists have been concerned that human activities could cause more plants and animals to become extinct than any point in the past. Along with human-made changes in climate (see above), some of these extinctions could be caused by overhunting, overfishing, invasive species, or habitat loss. A study published in May 2017 in <i><a href="/wiki/Proceedings_of_the_National_Academy_of_Sciences_of_the_United_States_of_America" title="Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences of the United States of America">Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences</a></i> argued that a “biological annihilation” akin to a sixth mass extinction event is underway as a result of anthropogenic causes, such as <a href="/wiki/Human_overpopulation" title="Human overpopulation">over-population</a> and <a href="/wiki/Overconsumption" title="Overconsumption">over-consumption</a>. The study suggested that as much as 50% of the number of animal individuals that once lived on Earth were already extinct, threatening the basis for human existence too.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sutter_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sutter-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Future_biosphere_extinction">Future biosphere extinction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction_event&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Future biosphere extinction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Future_of_Earth" title="Future of Earth">Future of Earth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fermi_paradox" title="Fermi paradox">Fermi paradox</a></div>
<p>The eventual warming and expanding of the Sun, combined with the eventual decline of atmospheric carbon dioxide could actually cause an even greater mass extinction, having the potential to wipe out even microbes, where rising global temperatures caused by the expanding Sun will gradually increase the rate of weathering, which in turn removes more and more carbon dioxide from the atmosphere. When carbon dioxide levels get too low (perhaps at 50 ppm), all plant life will die out, although simpler plants like grasses and mosses can survive much longer, until CO<sub>2</sub> levels drop to 10 ppm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Franck2005_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Franck2005-101">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward2003_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ward2003-102">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>With all photosynthetic organisms gone, atmospheric oxygen can no longer be replenished, and is eventually removed by chemical reactions in the atmosphere, perhaps from volcanic eruptions. Eventually the loss of oxygen will cause all remaining aerobic life to die out via asphyxiation, leaving behind only simple anaerobic prokaryotes. When the Sun becomes 10% brighter in about a billion years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Franck2005_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-Franck2005-101">[100]</a></sup> Earth will suffer a moist greenhouse effect resulting in its oceans boiling away, while the Earth's liquid outer core cools due to the inner core's expansion and causes the Earth's magnetic field to shut down. In the absence of a magnetic field, charged particles from the Sun will deplete the atmosphere and further increase the Earth's temperature to an average of ~420 K (147 °C, 296 °F) in 2.8 billion years, causing the last remaining life on Earth to die out. This is the most extreme instance of a climate-caused extinction event. Since this will only happen late in the Sun's life, such will cause the final mass extinction in Earth's history (albeit a very long extinction event).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Franck2005_101-2"><a href="#cite_note-Franck2005-101">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward2003_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ward2003-102">[101]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_and_recovery">Effects and recovery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction_event&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Effects and recovery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The impact of mass extinction events varied widely. After a major extinction event, usually only <a href="/wiki/Weed#Weeds_as_adaptable_species" title="Weed">weedy species</a> survive due to their ability to live in diverse habitats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quammen_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Quammen-103">[102]</a></sup> Later, species diversify and occupy empty niches. Generally, <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a> recovers 5 to 10 million years after the extinction event. In the most severe mass extinctions it may take 15 to 30 million years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quammen_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-Quammen-103">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>The worst event, the <a href="/wiki/Permian%E2%80%93Triassic_extinction_event" title="Permian–Triassic extinction event">Permian–Triassic extinction event</a>, devastated life on earth and is estimated to have killed off over 90% of species. Life seemed to recover quickly after the P-T extinction, but this was mostly in the form of <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_organism" title="Pioneer organism">disaster taxa</a>, such as the hardy <i><a href="/wiki/Lystrosaurus" title="Lystrosaurus">Lystrosaurus</a></i>. The most recent research indicates that the specialized animals that formed complex ecosystems, with high biodiversity, complex food webs and a variety of niches, took much longer to recover. It is thought that this long recovery was due to the successive waves of extinction which inhibited recovery, as well as to prolonged environmental stress to organisms which continued into the Early Triassic. Recent research indicates that recovery did not begin until the start of the mid-Triassic, 4M to 6M years after the extinction;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LehrmannRamezanBowring2006TimingOfRecovery_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-LehrmannRamezanBowring2006TimingOfRecovery-104">[103]</a></sup> and some writers estimate that the recovery was not complete until 30M years after the P-T extinction, i.e. in the late Triassic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SahneyBenton2008RecoveryFromProfoundExtinction_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-SahneyBenton2008RecoveryFromProfoundExtinction-105">[104]</a></sup> Subsequent to the P-T extinction, there was an increase in provincialization, with species occupying smaller ranges – perhaps removing incumbents from niches and setting the stage for an eventual rediversification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sidor2013_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sidor2013-106">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>The effects of mass extinctions on plants are somewhat harder to quantify, given the biases inherent in the plant fossil record. Some mass extinctions (such as the end-Permian) were equally catastrophic for plants, whereas others, such as the end-Devonian, did not affect the flora.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cascales-Mi.C3.B1ana2011_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cascales-Mi.C3.B1ana2011-107">[106]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction_event&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dissolved_oxygen" title="Dissolved oxygen">Dissolved oxygen</a> became more widespread and penetrated to greater depths; the development of life on land reduced the run-off of nutrients and hence the risk of <a href="/wiki/Eutrophication" title="Eutrophication">eutrophication</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anoxic_event" title="Anoxic event">anoxic events</a>; and marine ecosystems became more diversified so that <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">food chains</a> were less likely to be disrupted.</span></li>
</ol>

<div style="font-size:86%; position:relative; left:-1em; overflow:visible; white-space:nowrap">−500</div>
</div>
<div style="float:left;overflow:visible;width:8.3333333333333%">│
<div style="font-size:86%; position:relative; left:-1em; overflow:visible; white-space:nowrap">−450</div>
</div>
<div style="float:left;overflow:visible;width:8.3333333333333%">│
<div style="font-size:86%; position:relative; left:-1em; overflow:visible; white-space:nowrap">−400</div>
</div>
<div style="float:left;overflow:visible;width:8.3333333333333%">│
<div style="font-size:86%; position:relative; left:-1em; overflow:visible; white-space:nowrap">−350</div>
</div>
<div style="float:left;overflow:visible;width:8.3333333333333%">│
<div style="font-size:86%; position:relative; left:-1em; overflow:visible; white-space:nowrap">−300</div>
</div>
<div style="float:left;overflow:visible;width:8.3333333333333%">│
<div style="font-size:86%; position:relative; left:-1em; overflow:visible; white-space:nowrap">−250</div>
</div>
<div style="float:left;overflow:visible;width:8.3333333333333%">│
<div style="font-size:86%; position:relative; left:-1em; overflow:visible; white-space:nowrap">−200</div>
</div>
<div style="float:left;overflow:visible;width:8.3333333333333%">│
<div style="font-size:86%; position:relative; left:-1em; overflow:visible; white-space:nowrap">−150</div>
</div>
<div style="float:left;overflow:visible;width:8.3333333333333%">│
<div style="font-size:86%; position:relative; left:-1em; overflow:visible; white-space:nowrap">−100</div>
</div>
<div style="float:left;overflow:visible;width:8.3333333333333%">│

</div>
<div style="float:left;overflow:visible;width:0%">│
<div style="font-size:86%; position:relative; left:-1em; overflow:visible; white-space:nowrap">0</div>
